♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ [1,700,000-1,900,000] ♥km squared. For this estimate I have used the approximate territorial extent of the Mali Empire at its largest.
♠ Capital ♣ Niani ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Capital Niani in the core region of Kangaba. This was located near the Niger river - according to map, very near the source.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Capital city referred to as Mali.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_MlMali*] same place as Niani?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Sorotomo is the first major settlement within Mali's core territory to be dated conclusively to the period of the historical empire. Niani, Mali's speculative first capital, has only furnished C14 dates from before or after the key 13th to 15th-century period. This indicates that, at best, it was a late imperial seat of power. The issue of ancient Mali's capital has thus been a matter of much debate - and is as yet unresolved. There is currently general agreement among historians that Mali's first centre(s) of power would have been located in the so-called 'Pays Manding', c.200km south-east of Sorotomo. There is, however, substantial historical evidence suggesting a rapid shift of power towards the north-east during the empire's apogee."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_MlMali*]
♠ Language ♣ Mande; Arabic ♥ mid-14th century onwards? "Arabic became important both for the diffusion of religion and for communications and trade."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_MlMali*] mid-14th century onwards? Arabic "was used for official correspondence in the Ghana Empire before the end of the twelfth century and in Mali in the mid-fourteenth century."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_MlMali*]♠ Linguistic family ♣ ♥Mande language. However, there are regional variations. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Bamana[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Malinke ("people of Mali")[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Maninka (northeastern Guinea)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Mandinka (Senegal, Gambia, Guinea-Bissau "people of Mande")[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Dyula ("trader" northern Ivory Coast)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Soninke - founders of Ghana Empire
♠ Religion Genus ♣ Islam ♥ "In the Mali empire the Islamization of its rulers occurred at the end of the seventh/thirteenth century under the descendants of Sundiata. Although Ibn Battuta and Ibn Khaldûn maintain that this founding hero was converted to Islam, the Malinke oral tradition vehemently insists on his character as a 'pagan' magician and denies any conversion. But already Sundiata's son and successor Mansa Uli performed the pilgrimage during the reign of the Mamluk sultan, Baybars (658/1260-676/1277). Under his rule Mali expanded over the Sahel and took control of the trading towns of Waläta, Timbuktu and Gao, thus coming into a more direct contact with Islamized peoples than in the centuries before. From his reign onwards the royal pilgrimage became a permanent tradition among the mansas. The Islamic outlook of the empire took shape in the eighth/fourteenth century under Mansa Musa (c. 712/1312-738/1337) and his brother Mansa Sulaymän (f. 738/1341-761/1360) who encouraged the building of mosques and the development of Islamic learning. Ibn Battuta, an eye-witness, praised the zeal of the Malian Muslims in memorizing the Qoran and in attendance at the public prayers. The general feeling one has in reading his account is that Mali in the mid-eighth/fourteenth century was a country in which Islam had already taken root and whose inhabitants followed the main Islamic precepts. He does not mention any pagan religious practices and apart from the nakedness of women he did not observe anything prohibited by Islamic law." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_MlMali*] However, Niane [MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_MlMali*] writes that, when traveller Ibn Battuta visited this polity later in the fourteenth century, he noted that " the emperor also remained faithful to certain pagan customs, and Ibn Battuuta was shocked by many unorthodox practices. Apart from the presence of Arabs and the slight Muslim veneer, what happened at the court of the mansa differed very little from what might have been seen at the courts of non-Muslim kings, for example those of Mossi" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_MlMali*].

♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥After the gradual decline of the Ghana Empire, the power vacuum left in the Sudanese region was filled with several smaller successor states, including the Sosso Kingdom.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_MlMali*] In the early 13th century CE, several Malinke chiefdoms from the Upper Niger region united against the Sosso and slowly aggregated into what would become the Mali Empire.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_MlMali*] This polity, also known as the Mandingo Empire,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_MlMali*] was the largest of the West African empires, and flourished from the early 13th to the late 14th/early 15th century, at which point it started to decline.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_MlMali*] The apogee of the Mali Empire corresponds to the reign of Musa I of the Keita dynasty, the mansa (emperor) who reigned over 24 cities and their surrounding territories from 1312 to 1337.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_MlMali*] His empire extended from the Atlantic to Gao and the Niger Inland Delta, and from the southern Sahara to the tropical forest belt.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_MlMali*] Musa I is also famed for his patronage of Islam in Mali and for his lavish distribution of gold when he set off on a pilgrimage to Mecca in 1325.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The kings of the Keita dynasty sat at the apex of a confederation incorporating smaller kingdoms such as Ghana and Mema.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_MlMali*] At its height, the empire comprised 12 provinces made up of smaller, village-centred clan units.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_MlMali*] The mansa (emperor) was thus a 'chief of chiefs', assuming the mantle of a supreme patriarch, and he could dispense justice personally.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_41_MlMali*] He received advice from the griot, chosen from the Kouyate clan, who was also his spokesman and the tutor of princes.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_42_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The aristocracy formed around the Malinke warrior class,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_43_MlMali*] including an elite corps of cavalry.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_44_MlMali*] The empire maintained a strong army, with garrisons stationed in the main towns.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_45_MlMali*] The merchant class, known as Dyula or Wangara,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_46_MlMali*] formed settlements at the margins of the forest regions, such Kankan in modern-day Guinea, Bobo Dioulasso in modern Ivory Coast, and Begho in modern Ghana.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_47_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The cities of Mali were cosmopolitan, inhabited by people of every occupation and from every province of the empire,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_48_MlMali*] and prospering from their participation in Trans-Saharan trade networks and the export of gold, ivory, salt and slaves.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_49_MlMali*] Their characteristic mudbrick architecture, known as banco, can still be admired today.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_50_MlMali*] This distinctive architectural style is one of many signs of Mali's legacy in the region, as its language, laws and customs spread through West Africa. In the 15th century, however, a long period of gradual decline began. Timbuktu was captured by the Tuareg in 1433,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_MlMali*] and a few decades of internal political struggles made it difficult for the emperors to maintain control over such a large region, leading to the contraction of the empire's territory.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_52_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The empire was densely populated, with a reported 400 towns in the region and a compact net of villages near the trading city of Jenné.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_MlMali*] When the Andalusi diplomat Leo Africanus visited Niani in the 16th century, he described a thriving city of 'six thousand hearths'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_54_MlMali*]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [1,700,000-1,900,000] ♥ in squared kilometers[MAJIDBENAM_BR]1337 CE [MAJIDBENAM_REF_57_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Map with largest extent of the Mali Empire.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_58_MlMali*] expanded into Inland Delta, Gao, and eastern Songhai beginning 14th century. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_59_MlMali*] by mid-14th century mansa's effective rule limited to Mande homelands. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_60_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The boundaries of the Empire of Mali stretched from Kaoga (Gao) all the way to the Atlantic and from the Sahara to the tropical forest."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_61_MlMali*]
♠ Polity Population ♣ [4,000,000-5,000,000] ♥ People.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]40-50 million [MAJIDBENAM_REF_62_MlMali*] -- check (is reference correct? was it 4-5 million?). Reference checked, it was stated. However, it might be a typographical error. Population of Mali in 1960 was 5 million. No references in literature to massive population crash or genocide in the region between middle ages and 1960.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]McEvedy and Jones have the region of "The Sahel States" (Mauritania, Mali, Niger and Chad) at 2 million in 1000 CE, rising slowly to 3 million in 1500 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_63_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sahel states = Mauritania, Mali, Niger, Chad 2m by AD 1000[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Before the introduction of agriculture and animal husbandry the population of the area of the present-day Sahel states is unlikely to have exceeded 50,000: once pastoralism and agriculture had become well-established the population can hardly have been less than half a million. The chronology of the transition is as yet totally obscure, but there is no reason to postulate anything above the 50,000 line before 3000 BC or place the achievement of the half million later than 1000 BC. From this latter point a low rate of increase is all that is needed to bring the total to 1m by AD 1 and 2m by AD 1000." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_64_MlMali*]
♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ [12,000-17,000] ♥ Inhabitants.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Within the Mande-speaking heartland the basic building-block of government was the kafu, a community of anything from 1000 to 15,000 people living in or near a mud-walled town and ruled by a hereditary dynast called a fama." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_65_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Walata was a commercial city within the Mali Empire [MAJIDBENAM_REF_66_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Capital Niani, now lost, described in 16th century by Moroccan Leo Africanus as of 'six thousand hearths' while its inhabitants were 'the most civilised, intelligent and respected' in the Western Sudan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_67_MlMali*]
♠ Largest communication distance ♣ [800-1200] ♥ in kilometers.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Niani (capital) to Takedda (or Gao).

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 4 ♥ levels. Settlement hierarchy refers to the capital, important trading cities, mud-walled towns (“the basic building-block of government was the kafu, a community of anything from 1000 to 15,000 people living in or near a mud-walled town” ruled by a fama.), outlying villages who pay occasional tribute to the fama. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_68_MlMali*] The Mali empire was based upon a center-periphery political administration, in turn divided into three geopolitical sectors: provinces, districts, and village communities. The peripheral areas were composed of the conquered people of the tributary states and were ruled indirectly. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_69_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Capital town e.g. Niani[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Commercial town e.g. Walata
3. Basic mud-walled small town called a kafu
4. Smaller settlements/villages
♠ Administrative levels ♣ [4-5] ♥ levels.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. King of kings (mansa)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] king had title Mansa [MAJIDBENAM_REF_70_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "As in other African empires, the supreme ruler was a king of kings." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_71_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The ruler "bore the military title of mansa, conqueror." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_72_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The mansas adopted the Ghanaian and Sudanic concepts of kingship to institutionalize their power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_73_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The rulers surrounded themselves with a bodyguard, servants, and elaborate ceremonies."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_74_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]

there was an "imperial council" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_75_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] late 14th century government characterised by rule of powerful government officials and a sidelined monarch [MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Government officials
After his pilgrimage to Cairo and Mecca "Mansa Musa returned to Mali with Arab and Berber adventurers to serve in his administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_77_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Santigui (master of the treasury) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Scribes
"court circle included clerics and lawyers literate in Arabic" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_MlMali*]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. State farms official
"Client clans, castes of dependent craftsmen, and people allied by marriage or by past service supported the ruler. Slaves and serfs worked in agricultural settlements to provide produce for the court, the army, and the administration." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] royal slaves worked in "settled colonies" in the inland delta region. "each had to produce a quota of grain for collection by boat at the appointed season."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_MlMali*]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
_Mande chiefdoms_[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Chief (fama) of a kafu (or kafts)
"Mande-speaking ethnic core" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Mande-speaking peoples lived in family and village units, the head of the family being both priest and chieftain. A group of villages in turn formed a kafts, or kafu, a community of 1,000 to 15,000 people living around a mud-walled town and ruled by a hereditary chieftain called a fama."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Within the Mande-speaking heartland the basic building-block of government was the kafu, a community of anything from 1000 to 15,000 people living in or near a mud-walled town and ruled by a hereditary dynast called a fama."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Mema was a province in the Mali Empire [MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] the paramount ruler "bore the military title of mansa, "conquerer", which underlined the reality that his dominion might expand or contract according to the range of his armed forces. Where the mansa's soldiers were no longer seen, there the kafus would soon resume their independence under their traditional famas." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Oral tradition "Sunjata Epic" says Mali Empire founded by Sunjata Keita. Initially there was a Mande Chiefdom in Farakoro. The chief had the title maghan. Sunjata "organized the soldiers of all the Mande chiefdoms into a powerful army. They went to war against Susu." The unified Mande chiefdoms formed the basis of the Mali Empire. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_87_MlMali*]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
3. Village headman
"Mande-speaking peoples lived in family and village units, the head of the family being both priest and chieftain. A group of villages in turn formed a kafts, or kafu, a community of 1,000 to 15,000 people living around a mud-walled town and ruled by a hereditary chieftain alled a fama."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_MlMali*]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]

[MAJIDBENAM_REF_91_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Mansa Musa reigned 1312-1337 CE. Ibn Kathir (c1300-c1374 CE) sad he ruled over 24 other kings. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_92_MlMali*] Al-Umari said Musa had "conquered 24 cities, each with its surrounding district with villages and estates" and that he had a palace [MAJIDBENAM_REF_93_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "In Mali, as in other African empires, the supreme ruler was essentially a paramount, a king of kings, the degree of whose authority varied greatly from one part of his dominions to another, according to the accessibility of each to the imperial armies and tax collectors." Ibn Battuta witnessed a ceremony in which both the Mansa and the lesser king had their own personal guards of honor. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_102_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Oral tradition "Sunjata Epic" says Mali Empire founded by Sunjata Keita. Initially there was a Mande Chiefdom in Farakoro. The chief had the title maghan. There were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future." The chiefdom was conquered by Susu. Sunjata "organized the soldiers of all the Mande chiefdoms into a powerful army. They went to war against Susu." The unified Mande chiefdoms formed the basis of the Mali Empire. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_103_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "In each kingdom, each nation, the army was divided into several corps assigned to the defense of different provinces, although under the command of the civil authority. Thus, each provincial governor had at his disposal a part of this army which he could assign tasks under the orders of a general whose powers were purely military."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Individual soldier

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ inferred present ♥ late 14th century government characterised by rules of powerful government officials and a "puppet" monarch. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_MlMali*] We know that under the Songhai Askias the bureaucracy became very well developed but it is possible more rudimentary aspects of the following description preexisted under the earlier Empires. During the Songhai period, each high official was directly appointed by the king[MAJIDBENAM_REF_117_MlMali*], was given a "distinctive uniform and insignia" which were worn at royal audiences, and they sat together in a highly-formalised assembly.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_118_MlMali*] For the Mali Empire specifically we have references to the following officials: the hari-farma managed fishing on the Niger river.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_119_MlMali*] The sao-farma managed the forests.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_120_MlMali*] The babili-farma was minister of agriculture.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_121_MlMali*] The khalissi-farma was minister of finance.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_MlMali*] Reference for the later period Songhai state to prisons at Kanato, Kabara (near Timbuktu) and elsewhere[MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_MlMali*] where there was widespread use of "notarized documents" such as for an inventory of goods belonging to a prison inmate.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_MlMali*]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Mali rulers enforced customary law when it suited them and preserved ancient ceremonials." Some of the ruling classes and merchant classes were Muslim, everyone else pagan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_MlMali*] "It was customary for rulers of Western Sudan kingdoms to hold what were called audiences, during which ordinary citizens could submit complaints and legal disputes." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_MlMali*] They were held in public and one was witnessed by Ibn Battuta on his 1352-1353 visit. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Judges and legal experts. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_MlMali*] Islamic law. Judges chosen by Mansa. Mansa ultimate legal authority. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_130_MlMali*]

Because of a lack of materials to maintain this massive construction, it became a ruin and was only restored in the 16th century by El Aqib, using banco, the most typical construction material in Sudan. "Djinguereber: Longue de 85 m environ et large de 30 m, cette mosquée par ses dimensions montre bien que Hidji Mansa Moussa a voulu laisser là un monument digne de lui. Dans cette construction massive, comme le précédent monument, le matériau d'entretien fit défaut, c'est pour cette raison qu'il tomba en ruine et qu'au XVIè siècle El Aqib la restaura mais en utilisant le banco, matériau soudanais par excellence." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_146_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Jenné mosque is 11 m high and 56 m long. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_147_MlMali*]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ royal Hajj pilgrimage ♥ Between the thirteenth and the fourteenth century, three of Mali's most powerful rulers undertook the pilgrimage to the Hajj: Mansa Uti, Mansa Sankara and Mansa Musa. The latter's journey, in 1324, is particularly well documented, mostly because Arab chroniclers were struck Mansa Musa's enormous retinue and the large quantities of gold he spent in Egypt. Conrad (2001) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_244_MlMali*] writes that "the mansa was known for his spirit of penitence and devotion, and his pilgrimage was recorded in several Egyptian chronicles as one of the principal events of the year" and that "[i]t seems safe to say that this pilgrimage was similarly important for Mansa Musa's subjects back in Mali", and may have been at the root of legends regarding the region's synchretism between Islam and indigenous religious beliefs and practices. Having reached Mecca, it is likely that the Mali kings' ritual activity was broadly similar to the following description of contemporary practice[MAJIDBENAM_REF_245_MlMali*]: "In the pilgrimage to Mecca, the hajj, ancient Arab rites have been taken over and spiritualized. The pilgrimage is performed during the last lunar month, Dhu'l­hijja. People who come to Arabia at other times can perform the lesser pilgrimage, umra, which consists mainly of the circumambulation of the Ka'ba and the running between two hillocks called Safa and Marwa. Pilgrimage to a sacred place requires a ritual consecration. Thus, at a certain distance from Mecca the men, with shaven heads, put on a special garment, ihram, consisting of two white unsewn pieces of cloth that cover their whole body; women put on a covering garment. Pilgrims greet the sacred precincts with the call labbaika, "Here I am at Thy service!" The rites that have to be performed include the sevenfold circumambulation of the Ka'ba (where one tries to kiss the black stone in its southeastern corner) and a sevenfold running between Safa and Marwa, which are now connected by covered arcades. But the central rite is the "staying" on the hill 'Arafat*, a distance of some eight miles from Mecca, on the ninth day of the month. There, sermons are given, and on the way back the 'stoning of Satan' is performed by casting seven pebbles three times at a certain place. After that, on the tenth day of the month, each pilgrim slaughters a sheep or larger animal in Mina. Those staying home do the same. This part of the ritual commemorates the day when Abraham was ordered to sacrifice his son Isma'il, whose place was then taken by a ram. After the Feast of Offering the pilgrim can return from the sacred state, ihram, into the normal state, when the cutting of hair and nails, as well as sex, are again permitted. Killing any creatures inside the sacred area is considered a major sin. The pilgrimage should be performed only when the pilgrim is in good health and can undertake the journey without major difficulties, especially without incurring debts; this shows the realistic approach of Islam to religious practice. In former centuries the pilgrimage was a dangerous undertaking for those who came from distant lands, whether they traveled overland in the caravans which started, with great pomp, in Cairo or Baghdad, or sailed from India or Malaysia on fragile boats. (Air travel is not always very safe either.) Yet modern Muslims sometimes express the view that these slow, arduous journeys prepared them better for the great event than the brief trip by air, which barely leaves leisure to meditate on the experience of visiting the center of their religion. On their return the pilgrims bring a vessel filled with wholesome water from the well Zamzam, from which Hagar once drank. They are received by their families with pride and joy, and are called Hajji. Those who die during the pilgrimage are regarded as martyrs, shahid, "witness" for their faith. Doubtlessly the meeting of people from all parts of the world in Mecca and their common worship at the central sanctuary of Islam does much to strengthen the feeling of unity among Muslims. Through the centuries many scholars and pious people extended their stay in Mecca and exchanged their religious views with colleagues from other parts of the world. The sojourn near the Ka'ba inspired important theological and mystical works: the comprehensive commentary on the Koran by Zamakhshari (called by the honorific title Jar Allah, "God's neighbor" due to his prolonged stay in Mecca) grew out of the author's stay in Mecca. Even more evident is the influence of the Meccan experience on the most comprehensive work of Islamic theosophy, the Futuhat al­makkiyya, of Ibn 'Arabi, which was "revealed" to the author while he was circumambulating the Ka'ba. Furthermore, it is important to remember that almost all reform movements in the fringe areas of the Islamic world were triggered off by the pilgrimage. Faithful believers from Bengal and Nigeria, from Morocco and Central Asia, who had lived for some time in Mecca and exchanged their views with others who, like them, had experienced what seemed to be the 'true Arabic Islam', returned to their Page 39countries to fight the numerous indigenous superstitions and customs which had infiltrated the Islam of the masses and which now appeared to them like remnants of ancient paganism."
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ At Mecca itself [MAJIDBENAM_REF_246_MlMali*].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Mansa Musa undertook the pilgrimage in 1324 [MAJIDBENAM_REF_247_MlMali*], and, generally speaking, Muslims are only expected to visit the Hajj once in their lifetime.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_248_MlMali*]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ To witness a royal pilgrimage from Mali to the Hajj would have been a rare occurrence, as suggested by this quote: "Only kings whose authority was well established could be absent for such a long period, and only those whose kingdom prospered could undertake the cost" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_249_MlMali*].
♠ Duration ♣ [12-168] ♥ hours. More than a year. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_250_MlMali*] If we don't include the journey, the pilgrimage probably only lasted for a few days/weeks (here coded as an intermediate between a day and a week).

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ boliw worship ♥ Boliw worship was a key part of Mande ritual life in the twentieth century. It is not all certain that thirteenth- and fourteenth-century Mande would have also made offerings to boliw, or power objects, but widely known legends regarding the introduction of boliw rituals to the region links these practices to the time when Islam started to gain ground in the region [MAJIDBENAM_REF_289_MlMali*], which was around the time of the Mali Empire itself. The following is a description of boliw rituals in the twentieth century. "Fabricated by specialists, the most famous of whom live in the regions of Siguiri (Upper Guinea) and Segu (central Mali), Mande power objects are either inherited within the family or purchased by aspiring owners from a craft master at a price that may be quite elevated. In any event, the acquirer pledges to make offerings and to conduct his life in a particular way in relation to the object’s exigencies. Such requirements comprise, on the one hand, specific sacrificial requests—at particular times/spaces and of a particular nature (including substances such as animal blood, cola nuts, and millet beer)—and, on the other hand, more general obligations, usually regulating sexual activity, diet, and social behavior of the owner or tigi. Disobeying these requirements may result in the loss of the object’s power or, worse, in this power turning against the owner. An owner engages with the object chiefly through sacrifices performed both according to the annual cycle and in the time of need. They begin with formal address—a verbal reminder of the alliance uniting him with the object, usually including a thanksgiving for past assistance and a request for future protection. According to my field experience, then, the owner informs the object of his future plans (or of those of the relative, friend, or fellow villager for whom the sacrifice is being performed) to test the object’s view as to the probability of their realization. First the question is asked: “Will the person who promised me money keep the promise?” or “Will I kill some prey today?” The divination with cola nuts or other augurs—such as stones, cowry shells, poultry, or sand—follows, resulting in what is considered to be the object’s answer to the question. The actual sacrificial act functions within this schema either as the closing stage of the consultation or as the fulfillment of former promises made to the object, and always as a reenactment of the alliance between the object(s) and the human(s). [...] Regardless of its exact purpose, the object’s use is secret—i.e., under customary circumstances, one does not show power objects to others or willingly admit to owning them (although their existence is generally a “public secret”), nor does one publicly discuss their properties." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_290_MlMali*]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ monthly ♥ "An owner engages with the object chiefly through sacrifices performed both according to the annual cycle and in the time of need" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_291_MlMali*]. This suggests perhaps at least a monthly frequency.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ monthly ♥ "An owner engages with the object chiefly through sacrifices performed both according to the annual cycle and in the time of need" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_292_MlMali*]. This suggests perhaps at least a monthly frequency.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ "An owner engages with the object chiefly through sacrifices performed both according to the annual cycle and in the time of need" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_293_MlMali*]. This suggests perhaps at least a monthly frequency. (KB: Coded as not applicable as evidence below states ritual was carried out in secret)
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Half an hour. The ritual appears to be quite brief, comprising an offering, a simple conversation with the boliw, and a divination rite [MAJIDBENAM_REF_294_MlMali*].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers. Sources do not provide absolute numbers [MAJIDBENAM_REF_295_MlMali*].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ not applicable ♥ The ritual does not require a specialist [MAJIDBENAM_REF_296_MlMali*].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ The ritual is very widespread among the Mande[MAJIDBENAM_REF_297_MlMali*], though it is unclear whether Muslims would have performed as well in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries.
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ The ritual is carried out in secret [MAJIDBENAM_REF_298_MlMali*].[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Dysphoric elements

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Friday prayer ♥ "The Friday prayer is a duty of the community; it contains a short sermon, khutba*, which consists of two parts. It gives some advice usually based on the Koran or the hadith, and a prayer for the ruler of the government. To be mentioned in the khutba meant to be the legitimate ruler. Women rarely attend the Friday prayer but prefer to pray at home.The daily prayers are explained in the Koran as acts of humility and adoration. At the end of the prayer the Muslim can mention his/her personal requests. While traveling or in war or under duress, one can perform the noon and afternoon or sunset and night prayers together. One also has numerous opportunities to perform additional prayers of two or more rak'a each, e.g., during solar or lunar eclipses; when performing the rain prayer; when entering or leaving the house or the mosque—in short, some pious Muslims use every opportunity to perform a brief salat. The belief in the purifying power of ritual prayer is intense; the Prophet compared it to a stream of water that washes off sins five times a day. The performance of the prayer at the prescribed time constitutes ideally a means of educating Muslims to punctuality, cleanliness, and, since there is no ranking in the mosque, equal participation in the life of the community. Prayer can also lead to ecstatic experiences, and when one observes a praying Muslim who is oblivious of everything around him and seems to have drawn himself, as it were, out of this world to stand humbly before the Lord, one realizes best whence Islam derives its vital strength.To perform the salat, a clean spot suffices, be it in the fields, in the train, or in one's shop or office. A small prayer rug guarantees the cleanliness of the place. However, even in the Prophet's lifetime a place for congregational worship was established, called masjid, "the place where one prostrates" (hence the word Mosque). The great mosque, where the Friday prayer is held and which was found in each quarter, is called jami'*, "the gathering [mosque]."Mainly in the eastern part of the Islamic world, one finds small open prayer places called musalla*, and for the gathering of large congregations at the two 'id*, the end of Ramadan and the Feast of Offerings, one sometimes uses an 'idgah*, a vast enclosure with a prayer niche."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_331_MlMali*]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_332_MlMali*]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_333_MlMali*]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ weekly ♥ Every Friday. Smaller version five times a day.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_334_MlMali*]
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. Noon prayer on Fridays. Expert input needed for exact duration. at this time wiki scraper not able to read decimal, so cannot code less than 1 hour e.g. 0.05 for 15 minutes.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priesthood. Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ All Muslims, though women would frequently pray in private instead.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_335_MlMali*]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥Dysphoric elements
♠ fasting ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_336_MlMali*]
♠ vigil/sleep deprivation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_337_MlMali*]
♠ fear ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_338_MlMali*]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Id al Fitr ♥ 'Id al-Fitr. Ibn Battuta left an eyewitness account of the 'Id al-Fitr as it was celebrated during the reign of Mansa Musa, in the early fourteenth century: "The people come out, dressed in their best white clothes, to the place of prayer, which is close to the palace of the Sultan. The Sultan came mounted . . . with the qhadi, the khatib [preacher], and the fuquha [jurists] in front of him, calling la illaha illa llah ['There is no God but Allah], and allahu akbar ['Allah is Great']. In front of him were red silken flags. The sultan entered a tent, which had been pitched close to the place of prayer. After the prayer and the sermon had been accomplished, the preacher came down, sat in front of the Sultan, and made a long speech. There was a man, holding a spear in his hand, who explained the preacher's speech to the people in their language. The speech was an admonition and a warning to the people; it praised the Sultan and urged his people to obey him and to fulfil their obligations to him." After this, the Sultan sits on a dais, surrounded by his army officers, his counsellors, and representatives of the Islamic clergy, as his head griot (a sort of musician/bard/entertainer/jester figure) plays music and "sings a song praising the king, an account of his wars and deeds", accompanied, both musically and vocally, by his own several wives and concubines. Once the head griot had finished, lesser griots took the stage, dressed as birds, and recited poetry recounting the Sultan's genealogy, and reminding the Sultan to take on the example of his ancestors. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_368_MlMali*]. From contemporary descriptions of the festival, we can also say that the festival was celebrated once a year, at the end of Ramadan, and that it lasted three days; moreover, among the Empire's Muslims, it would have involved visiting friends and family, sharing food with them, and giving to the poor [MAJIDBENAM_REF_369_MlMali*].

♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_372_MlMali*]
♠ Duration ♣ 72 ♥ hours. Three days. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_373_MlMali*]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ circumcision ♥ There are no descriptions of circumcision rituals in the Mali Empire, but MacNaughton (1988) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_407_MlMali*] describes the circumcision ceremony as it occurred in Mande communities in the twentieth century. The ceremony was preceded a night of continuous dancing for the whole of the community. At the ceremony itself, a blacksmith was tasked with performing the surgery. Following the ceremony, the boys were kept in isolation and instructed regarding their new role in society: they had ceased being mere children, and become adult men. Societies of mutual support formed by men who had undergone circumcision together were a key institution in the Bamana Empire of the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries[MAJIDBENAM_REF_408_MlMali*], and it is possible, and perhaps even likely, that similar societies existed in the Mali Empire as well.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ every three years. "In general, operations are scheduled every three years, but in some communities they occur more frequently" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_409_MlMali*].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ "In general, operations are scheduled every three years, but in some communities they occur more frequently" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_410_MlMali*].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ every three years. "In general, operations are scheduled every three years, but in some communities they occur more frequently" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_411_MlMali*].
♠ Duration ♣ [20-36] ♥ The surgery itself takes probably between a few hours and a day, depending on the number of boys circumscribed, but it is preceded by a night of continuous dancing, and followed by two months of ritual isolation for the boys [MAJIDBENAM_REF_412_MlMali*].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers. Not indicated by the sources.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ local blacksmiths. Because of their ability to shape metal, blacksmiths are traditionally believed to have a number of strange powers, which often make them ideal ritual specialists [MAJIDBENAM_REF_413_MlMali*].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ boys in their late teens [MAJIDBENAM_REF_414_MlMali*]

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ "In Mali and Songhai "the king appinted the generals was himself commander-in-chief of the army and personally directed military operations".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_449_MlMali*] Askia Muhammad of Songahai Empire was in charge of a professional military: "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_450_MlMali*] After victory in 1235 CE there was a Gbara - Great Assembly - of the allies to decide the constitution. Empire divided into two regions (north "Faran Sura" and south "Sangar Zuma") under two generals."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_451_MlMali*]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred absent ♥ In reference to following Songhai Empire, author makes reference to "seat in the Assembly" in context of high officials.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_452_MlMali*] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_453_MlMali*] It was not until Askia Mohammed of the Songhai Empire when Islamic law (of the cities) became dominant over traditional African law (of the countryside. Before Askia Mohammed the kings were lenient toward non-Islamic religions/laws. In the Mali state a military aristocracy formed a council which advised the Emperor.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_454_MlMali*]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ present ♥ For Mali Empire we have al-Omari of Cairo who noted that Mansa Kankan Musa of Mali (1412-1337 CE) received legal cases and appeals and 'examined them himself'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_455_MlMali*] However, it seems that judicial power at the highest level may have been split. "Crimes of lese-majeste and high treason were under the jurisdiction of the king."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_456_MlMali*] The cadi, a Muslim judge appointed by the king, "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_457_MlMali*] Underscoring the independence of the judiciary, "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_458_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ "When the emperor arrived behind the window, Dugha served as mouthpiece, transmitting the orders, recovering the grievances and submitting them to the sovereign, who gave his decisions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_459_MlMali*]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_460_MlMali*] "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_461_MlMali*]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as stricly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual ersons, than were the governments of Europe."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_462_MlMali*] non-Muslim people in the countryside, people of the towns (i.e. merchant class?)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥

The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_463_MlMali*]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ non-Muslim people in the countryside, people of the towns (i.e. merchant class?). Songhai Empire period: "Askia Muhammad made a sharp break with the religious and family traditions of the Sunni line of rulers. He based his power firmly on the towns, and, in line with this, ruled as a strict Muslim."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_464_MlMali*]
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ ♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as strictly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual persons, than were the governments of Europe."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_465_MlMali*] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_466_MlMali*]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ General reference to divisions in societies of West Africa "veritical divisions which separated all the people of one clan from the people of other clans, all the inhabitants of one village from their neighbours, all the descenants of one set of ancestors from the descendants of other ancestors. These vertical, or 'up-and-down', divisions were in some ways even more important to West African social development that the horizontal divisions ... and they were already very ancient by the sixteenth century."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_467_MlMali*] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_468_MlMali*] For example, after 1591 CE the ulama of Timbuktu "backed the Moroccans against the Songhay rulers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_469_MlMali*]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ ♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_470_MlMali*] General reference to diviners and priests. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_471_MlMali*] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_472_MlMali*] For example, after 1591 CE the ulama of Timbuktu "backed the Moroccans against the Songhay rulers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_473_MlMali*] The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_474_MlMali*] "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_475_MlMali*] Askia Mohammed requested a "Sherif, i.e., the descendant of the Prophet" to be sent to live with in the Sudan. Caliph Mulay Abbas obliged and the Sherif became an important figure, "exempt from all the duties of citizenship (taxes, etc.), but they received gifts of impressive value."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_476_MlMali*]

♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_482_MlMali*] General reference to diviners and priests. ♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ absent ♥
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ "Like the Europeans of the Middle Ages (AD 600-1350), Africans lived in an 'age of faith'. They believed, in short, that political authority came not from men or women but from God and the spirits. Those who exercised power on earth could do so, in other words, only if they were accepted as speaking and acting with the good will of their departed ancestors, who, in turn, were their protectors and helpers in the world of the spirits. Rulers could only rule if they were spiritually appointed to do so; and their subjects obeyed them not simply from respect for the king's power and law, but also for reasons of religion."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_487_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Hereditary. Son followed father, e.g. Sundiata followed by his son Uli. Or brothers of king: Uli was followed by his brothers Wati and Khalifa.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_488_MlMali*] After victory in 1235 CE there was a Gbara - Great Assembly - of the allies to decide the constitution. Sundiata made Emperor, succession would be hereditary (the fratrilineal aspect would not be observed).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_489_MlMali*]

Sakuru (1298-1308 CE) 7. Mansa Qu 8. Mansa Muhammad 9. Mansa Kankan Musa (-1337 CE). 10. Mansa Magha (c4 years) 11. Mana Suleyman (-1360 CE). 12. Mari-Diata (c13 years) 13. ? 14. 15... Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥Mali state was like ancient Ghana empire but on a "greater scale"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_496_MlMali*] but did not have a bureaucracy of the extent developed from Askia Mohammed of the Songhai Empire. The extent of the government developed by later Songhai Askias can be appreciated in the fact there were more civil chiefs than military ones[MAJIDBENAM_REF_497_MlMali*] so before this period the government was more closely connected to the military. In the Mali state a military aristocracy formed a council which advised the Emperor.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_498_MlMali*] Head of government was called "Mansa". The griot was the Emperor's spokesperson.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_499_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]An indirect measure of the state's level of sophistication, by time of king Musa Mali had "enclosed a large part of the Western Sudan within a single system of law and order. He did this so successfully that the Morocaan writer Ibn Babuta, travelling through Mali about twelve years after Musa's death, found 'complete and general safety in the land'".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_500_MlMali*] "Mali ambassadors were established in Morocco, Egypt, and elsewhere"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_501_MlMali*] and might be considered state officials. While we have a reference that says top officials in Niani were mostly "noblemen" from aristocratic families[MAJIDBENAM_REF_502_MlMali*] the learned men around the king might also have been recruited from abroad: new mosques at Gao and Timbuktu and a palace were designed by as-Saheli, one of the 'learned men' Musa brought with him on return from Egypt.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_503_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Like the Songhai Empire that came after it the Mali state was run on the resources of slaves tribes and on a system of customs duties and taxation. The ruling family owned slave tribes: they were functionally specialised and tasked to produce crops to feed king's army, fodder for his horses, or grow or farm produce, like fish; other tribes were blacksmiths ("of foreign extraction"); five tribes were obliged to provide "one hundred spears and one hundred arrows per family each year" for the army; from one tribe the king recruited "domestic servants and confidential special emissaries" (but otherwise was exempt from a tax).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_504_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The slave tribes "had all the external appearances of a clan or tribe, as to number, identity of name, and collateral relationships. Yet they totally lacked the social structure and organization of the clan."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_505_MlMali*] We know in reference to the Sonhai Empire plantations worked by slaves were managed by a fanfa (slave-master or ship-master) who might be responsible for 100, 60, 50, 40, or 20 slaves.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_506_MlMali*] A plantation in Dendi had 200 slaves and four fanfas under a leader called Missakulallah, who was also a slave.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_507_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In general terms, the West African states were ruled by a non-landowning who nobility lived on "the institution of a tax, first conceived of as a tithe, a ritual deduction on the wealth of all subjects".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_508_MlMali*] The state gained revenue from taxation on merchants in gold coins or goods[MAJIDBENAM_REF_509_MlMali*] and from customs duties on imports and exports, which had existed from the Ghanaian period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_510_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Was there any court bureaucracy before Songhai?: "It is difficult to realize that, for fifteen hundred years, the Tunkas, Mansas, and Askias simply issued verbal orders and got verbal accounts and reports in return. The customs activities of the border areas, based on what we have just said, would presuppose some kind of precise accounting, just as the payment of taxes and other fees would seem to imply the issuing of receipts, especially to the peripatetic merchants at the markets of Timbuktu, Djenne, etc."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_511_MlMali*] Mali had a monopoly on gold production, especially lands of Wangara and Bambuk.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_512_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]We know that under the Songhai Askias the bureaucracy became very well developed but it is possible more rudimentary aspects of the following description preexisted under the earlier Empires. During the Songhai period, each high official was directly appointed by the king[MAJIDBENAM_REF_513_MlMali*], was given a "distinctive uniform and insignia" which were worn at royal audiences, and they sat together in a highly-formalised assembly.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_514_MlMali*] The king wore a headdress at royal audiences[MAJIDBENAM_REF_515_MlMali*] and his rank obligated him to speak softly so "the Mansa of Mali, like the Askia, and present-day marabouts, all had their heralds who audibly transmitted to the assembly the words of the chief."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_516_MlMali*] Court ceremony very similar throughout Black Africa: for example, covering the head with dust presented a sign of humility.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_517_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Written language was the culture of an urban élite, that did not absorb surrounding cultures and languages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_518_MlMali*] The development of the bureaucracy in the Western Sudan was thus linked to arabic writing and the acceptance of Islamic religion. The oldest example of writing in West Africa is from a 1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_519_MlMali*] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_520_MlMali*] In the Western Sudan "the customs of Islam helped towards the formation of regular civil services staffed by 'king's men': by officials, that is, who owed their appointments not to their birth but to individual skill and service to the king."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_521_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]In addition to Islam, a literate bureaucracy may also have been long encouraged by merchant habits. "The customs activities of the border areas, based on what we have just said, would presuppose some kind of precise accounting, just as the payment of taxes and other fees would seem to imply the issuing of receipts, especially to the peripatetic merchants at the markets of Timbuktu, Djenne, etc."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_522_MlMali*] The merchant wealth may be connected to the practice of payment for office: "Appointments to the various offices, in the traditional kingdoms, entailed the payment of a customary fee, not, however, necessarily to the king. ... Later on, with the profanation of the royal office, its secularization, this fee would return to the king. Thus, in Songhai, at the time of the conflict with Morocco, a Fondoko upon appointment was required to turn over two thousand cows."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_523_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]For the Mali Empire specifically we have references to the following officials: the hari-farma managed fishing on the Niger river.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_524_MlMali*] The sao-farma managed the forests.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_525_MlMali*] The babili-farma was minister of agriculture.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_526_MlMali*] The khalissi-farma was minister of finance.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_527_MlMali*] Reference for the later period Songhai state to prisons at Kanato, Kabara (near Timbuktu) and elsewhere[MAJIDBENAM_REF_528_MlMali*] where there was widespread use of "notarized documents" such as for an inventory of goods belonging to a prison inmate.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_529_MlMali*]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 12 ♥At its height empire had 12 provinces. Provincial government small scale model of central government. Provinces had sub-divisions, sometimes these were clans, usually under a village chief (dugu-tigi).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_551_MlMali*]
♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥After victory in 1235 CE there was a Gbara - Great Assembly - of the allies to decide the constitution. Allied chiefs would retain their provinces. In the conquered provinces, the chiefs would be assisted by a governor (farin). Existing provinces retained their institutions.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_552_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Head of government called "Mansa". Military aristocracy formed a council which advised the Emperor. The griot was the Emperor's spokesperson. Village was basis of political structure. Group of villages made a province (kafu or jamana), under a chief.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_553_MlMali*] At its height empire had 12 provinces. Provincial government small scale model of central government. Provinces had sub-divisions, sometimes these were clans, usually under a village chief (dugu-tigi).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_554_MlMali*] "Each provincial governor in Black Africa was an image of the king, with his own small court."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_555_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Empire also divided into two regions (north "Faran Sura" and south "Sangar Zuma") under two generals. Rights and duties of each clan were decided, which included the tradition of "craft clans."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_556_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]According to Ibn Battuta, around 1352 CE "The broader regions of the Empire, such as Ualata, at the edge of the Sahara, were governed by Black farbas who levied customs duties and other taxes on caravans bringing merchandise into the country. Upon arrival, the marchants had to clear administrative formalities with them, before being allowed to carry on their trade."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_557_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]General reference to West African states and empires: "Their rulers governed through a system of governors and sub-governors; and each of these had his own bureaucracy ... These office-holders or officials operated an early form of civil service. ... This started a long time ago, certainly in the days of Ancient Ghana."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Courts in polity capital ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_607_MlMali*] Capital city had an Arab quarter with mosques and Muslim jurists.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_608_MlMali*] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_609_MlMali*] "When the emperor arrived behind the window, Dugha served as mouthpiece, transmitting the orders, recovering the grievances and submitting them to the sovereign, who gave his decisions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_610_MlMali*] Capital city had an Arab quarter with mosques and Muslim jurists.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_611_MlMali*] anfara-kuma: "traditional judge in pre-Islamic times. It was a hereditary office always held by members of the Kuma clan. They were anfaras, or judges, whence the term anfara-kuma which came to mean the cadi in Songhai.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_612_MlMali*] The Tarik el Fettach said Askia Mohammed (Songhai ruler) "appointed all the cadis in his kingdom: 'Thus it was that he appointed a cadi at Timbuktu, a cadi in the city of Djenne, and a cadi in each city of his territory which had one, from the Kanta to the Sibiridugu."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_613_MlMali*] The Tarikh es Sudan, however, claimed the cadis of Timbuktu was the most important judge and that he could remove those subordinate him - perhaps this work was referring to the period before the centralizing reforms of Askia Mohammed? (Songhai ruler)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_614_MlMali*]
♠ Courts in regional capitals ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_615_MlMali*] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_616_MlMali*] anfara-kuma: "traditional judge in pre-Islamic times. Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ inferred present ♥ "The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_622_MlMali*] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_623_MlMali*] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_624_MlMali*]

♠ Gender ♣ inferred absent ♥ Manden Charter of Kurukan Fuga dates to the thirteenth century and codified the Mandinka beliefs concerning human rights among polities stretching from the Niger River bend to the Atlantic Ocean from the 13th to 19th centuries. As a complete text, the charter was compiled from oral accounts, and establishes the concept of human rights predating both the Magna Carta and the Declaration of the Rights of Man, and contains over forty chapters advocating social peace in diversity, the inviolability of the human being, and more. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_646_MlMali*]
♠ Social class ♣ inferred present ♥ Under following Songhai state unfree women "forbidden to marry free men."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_647_MlMali*] Askia permitted unfree women to marry free men but stipulated that her children would remain enslaved.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_648_MlMali*]

The manipulation of the social order to reinforce the advantages of the nobility moreover had made the position of slave soldiers more fluid.” (68) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_670_MlMali*] Upward mobility likely possible through Islamic scholarly apparatus, however documentation of the rise of rank-and-file scholars is rare. Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ ♥"When Askia Mohammed defeated Sonni Baro Dau, the son of Sonni Ali, he took over some twenty-four slave tribes that belonged to him."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_678_MlMali*] As retold by Diop, according to the Tarikh el Fettach, "Before belonging to the Songhai, they too had first been the property of the king of Mali; starting only with Sonni Madogo did they change imperial masters. The first three tribes were of Bambara origin and had most likely not yet been converte to Islam, if Kati is to be believed; in other words, the slave might be of a religion other than that of his master. The king of Mali selected his domestic servants from among them. When one of the men wished to marry, the king would furnish a dowry of forty thousand cowries 'to the parents-in-law of the groom, so as to prevent the wife or children from claiming their freedom, and in order to make sure that they and their progeny would always remain the property of the Malli-koi'".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_679_MlMali*] Slave tribes 'obliged to pay an annual tribute of forty cubits [of cultivated land] per couple, man and wife'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_680_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Askia Daud (Songhai period) had plantations worked by slaves and managed by a fanfa (slave-master or ship-master) who might be responsible for 100, 60, 50, 40, or 20 slaves.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_681_MlMali*] A plantation in Dendi had 200 slaves and four fanfas under a leader called Missakulallah, who was also a slave.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_682_MlMali*] However, the slave was not bound to the soil[MAJIDBENAM_REF_683_MlMali*] "as long as they remained within the area; they were, more and more, sort of 'subjects' of the king. Their misfortune lay in the awful and hateful fact that they might at any moment be sold."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_684_MlMali*] That is the only sense in which they relate to the European slave of the time.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_685_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Generally in West African states: slaves sourced through warfare e.g. Muslim state raids non-muslim state for slaves, and vice versa.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_686_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]General reference to increasing stratification of societies in West Africa: "unfree servants or slaves" being the third tier of stratified society, below "free farmers" and "senior chiefs".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_687_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Generally in West African states (except Berber Mauritania): "Seldom if ever was it chattel slavery, ... Often it is scarcely deserved the name of slavery at all, being only a form of service which carried special duties and obligations. Slaves could easily work themselves into freedom."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_688_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Askia Daud in 1559 CE married Nara who is described as living a luxurious life "covered with jewels, surrounded by numerous saves, both male and female, and abundantly supplied with furniture and baggage. All of her household utensils were made of gold: dishes, jars, mortars, pestles, and so on."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_689_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_690_MlMali*] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_691_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"the Koran's point of view on this question is shaded. One may have a slave under the following conditions: to begin with, if he is a prisoner of a holy war - but then he must be educated, cared for, and converted; but, on the other hand, it is forbidden to take as a slave a Muslim as well educated as oneself; so a slave must be freed as soon as he reaches the intellectual level of his master."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_692_MlMali*]
♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as stricly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual ersons, than were the governments of Europe."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_693_MlMali*] There was no land holding class in 16th century West Africa.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_694_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]However, "many big political systems were founded" on a system of vassals (free men) who owed service to their lords who gave them protection.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_695_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]General reference for West African society: Unfree men "usually 'tied' to a certain place and occupation, and were forbidden to move away or change their work."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_696_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]General reference: "The African peasant's situation was therefore diametrically opposed to that of the serf bound to the soil and belonging, along with the land he cultivated, to a lord and master. The conditions under which the very first 'masters of the soil,' such as the Serer Lamans, ceded plots of land were in no way comparable to those in force during fuedal times: they could never result in the loss of liberty for a non-slave. Under the worst of them, they caled for an annual rental guaranteed by verbal agreement, cancellable at the end of each season. Even the poor worker, the navetane, who possessed only the strength of his own arms, could not be reduced to slavery. In the morning he worked for the Laman, and in the afternoon for himself, on the same plot granted to him."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_697_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Types

Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_712_MlMali*]
♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ inferred present ♥ "many big political systems were founded" on a system of vassals (free men) who owed service to their lords who gave them protection.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_713_MlMali*] General reference for West African society: "since nobody worked for money, free men as well as slaves had duties they could not escape".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_714_MlMali*] General reference: "The African peasant's situation was therefore diametrically opposed to that of the serf bound to the soil and belonging, along with the land he cultivated, to a lord and master. The conditions under which the very first 'masters of the soil,' such as the Serer Lamans, ceded plots of land were in no way comparable to those in force during fuedal times: they could never result in the loss of liberty for a non-slave. Under the worst of them, they caled for an annual rental guaranteed by verbal agreement, cancellable at the end of each season. Even the poor worker, the navetane, who possessed only the strength of his own arms, could not be reduced to slavery. In the morning he worked for the Laman, and in the afternoon for himself, on the same plot granted to him."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_715_MlMali*] "The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_716_MlMali*] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_717_MlMali*]

♠ Prisoners ♣ absent ♥ "“Our” present religions, Judaism, Christianity, and Islam, sometimes called Abrahamic religions, have the theme of human sacrifice right at their inception, with the founding father, Abraham. We are accustomed to the story, which remains a text of “terror and trembling,” as Kierkegaard put it. Abraham is commanded by god to “sacrifice” his only son Isaac, and he is willing to do so (Gen. 22). The Hebrew term for this sacrifice is colah, “make to rise upwards,” as fire and smoke rise to heaven;10 the action is to cut the throat with a knife and to burn the corpse on wood—the knife and wood expressly mentioned in the text. The human victim must be fettered in order to receive the deadly cut. Abraham does obey his god’s command, without even an attempt at protest. The text enjoys the paradox: “the son that you love”; “and the two walked together” (Gen. 22.1-19: 2; 8). In Muslim tradition, the place where Abraham “bound” his son is the rock on the temple hill of Jerusalem, which, since Khalif Omar, is covered by the “temple dome” with its golden cupola. Of course, an angel stops Abraham’s action at the last moment, pointing to an animal for sacrifice. The text says God was “testing” (nissah) Abraham, and his submissive obedience is highly rewarded. The text also states that god accepted the ram “instead of” (tachath) Isaac. One way to discard the terror of the story is to introduce a perspective of historical progress: God was replacing human sacrifice by animal sacrifice. This could be termed an advance in civilization. The Islamic interpretation comes close to this interpretation, as it makes this a founding myth for el eid." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_724_MlMali*]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥
♠ Expert ♣ ♥
♠ Name of Official Cult ♣ Islam ♥ "Reasons of state that led to a degree of Islamization in the formerly non-Muslim empires were both internal and external. The external ones were connected with trade since the function of these empires from the economic point of view was the control and exploitation of the Sudan's trade with North Africa. The ruling class had a real interest in presenting an Islamized front - by the organization of its courts and by performing the pilgrimage - in order to establish and improve good relations with its North African clients and partners. In the internal sphere one of the great problems of the imperial rulers was how to secure the allegiance of other subjected pagan clans and peoples which possessed totally different ancestor and land cults from those of the ruling dynasty. A universal religion such as Islam seemed to offer a suitable solution; an effort was made to implant it at least among the heads of other lineages and clans and to establish a new common religious bond. The increasing extent of the empires made the effective administration of the realm more complicated; in this respect the help of Muslim scribes and other literate persons was indispensable for correspondence and control of state affairs. The influence of Muslim clerics at the courts must have been great, thus preparing the ground for the ultimate conversion of the ruler and his family. "This does not mean that the kings were necessarily very devout or deep Muslims. They also had to reckon with the local customs and traditional beliefs of the majority of their non-Muslim subjects who looked upon the rulers as incarnations of or intermediaries of supranatural powers. None of the rulers had the political power to enforce Islam or Islamic law without compromising the loyalty of the non-Muslims. This helps to explain the numerous pagan rites and ceremonies at the courts of Muslim kings like the mansas of Mali and of the askiyas of Songhay, men who had performed the pilgrimage and were commonly considered to be devout Muslims. "In the Mali empire the Islamization of its rulers occurred at the end of the seventh/thirteenth century under the descendants of Sundiata. Although Ibn Battuta and Ibn Khaldûn maintain that this founding hero was converted to Islam, the Malinke oral tradition vehemently insists on his character as a 'pagan' magician and denies any conversion. But already Sundiata's son and successor Mansa Uli performed the pilgrimage during the reign of the Mamluk sultan, Baybars (658/1260-676/1277). Under his rule Mali expanded over the Sahel and took control of the trading towns of Waläta, Timbuktu and Gao, thus coming into a more direct contact with Islamized peoples than in the centuries before. From his reign onwards the royal pilgrimage became a permanent tradition among the mansas. The Islamic outlook of the empire took shape in the eighth/fourteenth century under Mansa Musa (c. 712/1312-738/1337) and his brother Mansa Sulaymän (f. 738/1341-761/1360) who encouraged the building of mosques and the development of Islamic learning. Ibn Battuta, an eye-witness, praised the zeal of the Malian Muslims in memorizing the Qoran and in attendance at the public prayers. The general feeling one has in reading his account is that Mali in the mid-eighth/fourteenth century was a country in which Islam had already taken root and whose inhabitants followed the main Islamic precepts. H e does not mention any pagan religious practices and apart from the nakedness of women he did not observe anything prohibited by Islamic law." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_729_MlMali*] However, Niane [MAJIDBENAM_REF_730_MlMali*] writes that, when traveller Ibn Battuta visited this polity later in the fourteenth century, he noted that " the emperor also remained faithful to certain pagan customs, and Ibn Battuuta was shocked by many unorthodox practices. Apart from the presence of Arabs and the slight Muslim veneer, what happened at the court of the mansa differed very little from what might have been seen at the courts of non-Muslim kings, for example those of Mossi" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_731_MlMali*]. In contrast with the previous quote, this suggests that using generic Islamic codes is not entirely adequate. Unfortunately, very little is known about the non-Islamic elements of this polity's normative ideology, as well as the normative ideology before the arrival of Islam.

Different religious customs and ceremonies were allowed at his court."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_732_MlMali*]
♠ Description of the Normative Ideology ♣ ♥

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; present: 1312-1410ce ♥ “In a departure from the principle of tawhid and thus from the belief that God governs the entire world, all spheres of life in the Islamic state are expected to be organized in accordance with Islamic revelation. In other words, political authority in Islam has always to be grounded in divine legitimacy.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_733_MlMali*][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Like the Europeans of the Middle Ages (AD 600-1350), Africans lived in an 'age of faith'. They believed, in short, that political authority came not from men or women but from God and the spirits. Those who exercised power on earth could do so, in other words, only if they were accepted as speaking and acting with the good will of their departed ancestors, who, in turn, were their protectors and helpers in the world of the spirits. Rulers could only rule if they were spiritually appointed to do so; and their subjects obeyed them not simply from respect for the king's power and law, but also for reasons of religion."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_734_MlMali*]
♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; absent: 1312-1410ce ♥ “In Islam all men are equal, whatever their colour, language, race or nationality. Islam addresses itself to the conscience of humanity and banishes all false barriers of race, status and wealth.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_735_MlMali*]
♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; absent: 1312-1410ce ♥ “As an uncompromising and pure monotheistic religion, according to Islam, God is one and unique (tawhid); and He has no partner, no children and no parents.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_736_MlMali*]
♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ inferred present: 1235-1311ce; uncertain_present_absent: 1312-1410ce ♥ Islam is monotheistic [MAJIDBENAM_REF_737_MlMali*] However, the founder of the Mali Empire is still worshipped, in what may or may not be a tradition that dates to his demise in the thirteenth century: "A number of villages maintain places of worship in memory of Sundiata: at Kirina on the Niger, the 'traditionalists', the Mamissoko, offer sacrifices to him in a sacred forest. North-east of Niani, at Tigan, there is a pile of ashes in Cámara country called 'Bundalin', under which there are believed to be shoes, a knife and a suit of armour which belonged to Sundiata. Then there is the ceremony held every seven years at Kangaba around the sanctuary called Kamablon, where there are also objects that belonged to Sundiata." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_738_MlMali*]
♠ Automatic deification ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; absent: 1312-1410ce ♥ Islam is monotheistic [MAJIDBENAM_REF_739_MlMali*]
♠ Rulers are gods ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; absent: 1312-1410ce ♥ Islam is monotheistic [MAJIDBENAM_REF_740_MlMali*]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ suspected unknown: 1235-1311ce; present: 1312-1410ce ♥ “In Islam all men are equal, whatever their colour, language, race or nationality. Islam addresses itself to the conscience of humanity and banishes all false barriers of race, status and wealth.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_741_MlMali*]
